<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequencing and population genomics modeling provide insights into the local adaptation of weeping forsythia"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-08-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Understanding the genetic basis underlying the local adaptation of nonmodel species is a fundamental goal in evolutionary biology. In this study, we explored the genetic mechanisms of the local adaptation of Forsythia suspensa using genome sequence and population genomics data obtained from specific-locus amplified fragment sequencing. We assembled a high-quality reference genome of weeping forsythia (Scaffold N50&#8201;=&#8201;7.3&#8201;Mb) using ultralong Nanopore reads. Then, genome-wide comparative analysis was performed for 15 natural populations of weeping forsythia across its current distribution range. Our results revealed that candidate genes associated with local adaptation are functionally correlated with solar radiation, temperature and water variables across heterogeneous environmental scenarios. In particular, solar radiation during the period of fruit development and seed drying after ripening, cold, and drought significantly contributed to the adaptive differentiation of F. suspensa. Natural selection exerted by environmental factors contributed substantially to the population genetic structure of F. suspensa. Our results supported the hypothesis that adaptive differentiation should be highly pronounced in the genes involved in signal crosstalk between different environmental variables. Our population genomics study of F. suspensa provides insights into the fundamental genetic mechanisms of the local adaptation of plant species to climatic gradients."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Understanding the genetic basis underlying the local adaptation of nonmodel species is a fundamental goal in evolutionary biology. In this study, we explored the genetic mechanisms of the local adaptation of <i>Forsythia suspensa</i> using genome sequence and population genomics data obtained from specific-locus amplified fragment sequencing. We assembled a high-quality reference genome of weeping forsythia (Scaffold N50â=â7.3âMb) using ultralong Nanopore reads. Then, genome-wide comparative analysis was performed for 15 natural populations of weeping forsythia across its current distribution range. Our results revealed that candidate genes associated with local adaptation are functionally correlated with solar radiation, temperature and water variables across heterogeneous environmental scenarios. In particular, solar radiation during the period of fruit development and seed drying after ripening, cold, and drought significantly contributed to the adaptive differentiation of <i>F. suspensa</i>. Natural selection exerted by environmental factors contributed substantially to the population genetic structure of <i>F. suspensa</i>. Our results supported the hypothesis that adaptive differentiation should be highly pronounced in the genes involved in signal crosstalk between different environmental variables. Our population genomics study of <i>F. suspensa</i> provides insights into the fundamental genetic mechanisms of the local adaptation of plant species to climatic gradients.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Spatial heterogeneity of the environment exerts differential selection pressures on natural populations, potentially leading to the local adaptation of a species across its range<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Kawecki, T. J. &amp; Ebert, D. Conceptual issues in local adaptation. Ecol. Lett.7, 1225â1241 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4090e477">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Cushman, S. A. et al. Editorial: the least cost path from landscape genetics to landscape genomics: challenges and opportunities to explore NGS data in a spatially explicit context. Front. Genet.9, 215 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4090e480">2</a></sup>. For naturally distributed plant species, selection pressure is mainly generated by abiotic factors such as soil chemistry, temperature, precipitation, and solar radiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Pereira, A. Plant abiotic stress challenges from the changing environment. Front. Plant Sci.7, 1123 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4090e484">3</a></sup>. These abiotic factors induce adaptive genetic differentiation on a genome-wide scale via spatially varying selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Rellstab, C. et al. A practical guide to environmental association analysis in landscape genomics. Mol. Ecol.24, 4348â4370 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4090e488">4</a></sup>. Adaptive divergence may lead to changes in gene expression, metabolism, physiology, phenotype, or phenology that significantly increase the adaptive ability of species across local environmental gradients<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Rellstab, C. et al. A practical guide to environmental association analysis in landscape genomics. Mol. Ecol.24, 4348â4370 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4090e492">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhu, J. K. Abiotic stress signaling and responses in plants. Cell167, 313â324 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4090e495">5</a></sup>. Identifying the regions at the genome involved in local adaptation can aid in the design of appropriate protection and management strategies to reduce the impact of future climate change, in addition to providing insights into the genetic mechanism of local adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Allendorf, F. W., Hohenlohe, P. A. &amp; Luikart, G. Genomics and the future of conservation genetics. Nat. Rev. Genet.11, 697â709 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4090e499">6</a></sup>. Genome scanning on a population scale enables the identification of genetic variants that control adaptive differences among natural populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Radwan, J. &amp; Babik, W. The genomics of adaptation. Proc. Biol. Sci.279, 5024â5028 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4090e504">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Li, Y. et al. Ten years of landscape genomics: challenges and opportunities. Front. Plant Sci.8, 2136 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4090e507">8</a></sup>.</p><p>With the rapid development of sequencing technology, it is now possible to generate genomic information to understand the genomic basis underlying local adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cushman, S. A. Grand challenges in evolutionary and population genetics: the importance of integrating epigenetics, genomics, modeling, and experimentation. Front. Genet.5, 197 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4090e514">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Guggisberg, A. et al. The genomic basis of adaptation to calcareous and siliceous soils in Arabidopsis lyrata. Mol. Ecol.27, 5088â5103 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4090e517">10</a></sup>. As a result of this genomics revolution, evidence of local adaptation has greatly increased in recent decades. Some notable examples include <i>Apis cerana</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Corymbia calophylla</i>, <i>Fundulus heteroclitus</i>, and <i>Peromyscus maniculatus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Brennan, R. S. et al. Integrative population and physiological genomics reveals mechanisms of adaptation in killifish. Mol. Biol. Evol.35, 2639â2653 (2018)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4090e536">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, C. et al. Population genomics provide insights into the evolution and adaptation of the eastern honey bee (Apis cerana). Mol. Biol. Evol.35, 2260â2271 (2018)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4090e536_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dittberner, H. et al. Natural variation in stomata size contributes to the local adaptation of water-use efficiency in Arabidopsis thaliana. Mol. Ecol.27, 4052â4065 (2018)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4090e536_2">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Pfeifer, S. P. et al. The evolutionary history of Nebraska deer mice: local adaptation in the face of strong gene flow. Mol. Biol. Evol.35, 792â806 (2018)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4090e536_3">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ahrens, C. W., Byrne, M. &amp; Rymer, P. D. Standing genomic variation within coding and regulatory regions contributes to the adaptive capacity to climate in a foundation tree species. Mol. Ecol.28, 2502â2516 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4090e539">15</a></sup>. These empirical studies identified adaptive genetic variation on a genome-wide scale that is driven by natural selection along environmental gradients.</p><p>Different environmental factors exert different selection pressures on species, which may be due to the differential sensitivity of species to these environmental factors and how environmental gradients interact with fitness landscapes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cushman, S. A. Grand challenges in evolutionary and population genetics: the importance of integrating epigenetics, genomics, modeling, and experimentation. Front. Genet.5, 197 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4090e546">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wright, S. Evolution in Mendelian populations. Genetics16, 97â159 (1931)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4090e549">16</a></sup>. This raises the fundamental question of which environmental factors play key roles in promoting adaptive divergence. Recent studies have suggested that environmental factors related to ecological niche dimensions are the main forces driving adaptive differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Miao, C. Y. et al. Landscape genomics reveal that ecological character determines adaptation: a case study in smoke tree (Cotinus coggygria Scop.). BMC Evol. Biol.17, 202 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4090e553">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Li, J. X. et al. Adaptive genetic differentiation in Pterocarya stenoptera (Juglandaceae) driven by multiple environmental variables were revealed by landscape genomics. BMC Plant Biol.18, 306 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4090e556">18</a></sup>.</p><p>However, the results supporting the habitat niche selection hypothesis have been produced using low-density molecular markers, and it is doubtful whether the same conclusion will be reached when analysis is extended to genome-wide markers. An additional critical question is which genes are involved in local adaptation. Recent population genomics studies in animals and humans have shown that only a small number of genes are typically involved in local adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Brennan, R. S. et al. Integrative population and physiological genomics reveals mechanisms of adaptation in killifish. Mol. Biol. Evol.35, 2639â2653 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4090e563">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Pfeifer, S. P. et al. The evolutionary history of Nebraska deer mice: local adaptation in the face of strong gene flow. Mol. Biol. Evol.35, 792â806 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4090e566">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Arciero, E. et al. Demographic history and genetic adaptation in the Himalayan region inferred from genome-wide SNP genotypes of 49 populations. Mol. Biol. Evol.35, 1916â1933 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4090e569">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Friis, G. et al. Genome-wide signals of drift and local adaptation during rapid lineage divergence in a songbird. Mol. Ecol.27, 746â760 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4090e572">20</a></sup>. Compared with animals and humans, plants often exhibit low migration abilities, which may increase the strength of local selection pressures in relation to climate change and environmental heterogeneity, resulting in more adaptive genetic variations in the genome to cope with these stresses.</p><p>The recent growth of plant population genomics has provided key information and perspectives that have revolutionized research on local adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ahrens, C. W., Byrne, M. &amp; Rymer, P. D. Standing genomic variation within coding and regulatory regions contributes to the adaptive capacity to climate in a foundation tree species. Mol. Ecol.28, 2502â2516 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4090e580">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Fitzpatrick, M. C. &amp; Keller, S. R. Ecological genomics meets community-level modelling of biodiversity: mapping the genomic landscape of current and future environmental adaptation. Ecol. Lett.18, 1â16 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4090e583">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Guerrero, J. et al. Soil environment is a key driver of adaptation in Medicago truncatula: new insights from landscape genomics. N. Phytol.219, 378â390 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4090e586">22</a></sup>. In some past studies of adaptive evolution, substantial effects of pleiotropy were found<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Keller, S. R. et al. Local adaptation in the flowering-time gene network of balsam poplar, Populus balsamifera L. Mol. Biol. Evol.29, 3143â3152 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4090e590">23</a></sup>. Moreover, many adaptive differentiation loci are frequently simultaneously associated with more than two environmental variables, which suggests that adaptive differentiation may occur more frequently in the genes showing crosstalk between different environmental modules.</p><p>Here, we propose the hypothesis that adaptive differentiation will occur at a high frequency in the genes responsible for signal crosstalk between different environmental variables during the process of local adaptation. However, most of the previous studies in this research area used molecular markers without sequence information or single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) markers without a reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Li, J. X. et al. Adaptive genetic differentiation in Pterocarya stenoptera (Juglandaceae) driven by multiple environmental variables were revealed by landscape genomics. BMC Plant Biol.18, 306 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4090e597">18</a></sup>. These limitations make it difficult to ascertain which genes generate adaptive differentiation for local adaptation and whether adaptive differentiation occurs in genes related to signal crosstalk between different environmental factors. Conducting adaptive genomics studies on species with available reference genomic information will better answer these questions and provide a clearer understanding of the genetic mechanisms of local adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Rellstab, C. et al. A practical guide to environmental association analysis in landscape genomics. Mol. Ecol.24, 4348â4370 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4090e601">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Manel, S. et al. Genome assemblies, genomic resources and their influence on the detection of the signal of positive selection in genome scans. Mol. Ecol.25, 170â184 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4090e604">24</a></sup>.</p><p>The last question that we investigate in this paper is whether the selection pressures imposed by these environmental gradients are sufficient to affect the population genetic structure of species. In the past, we used neutral molecular markers to investigate the population genetic structure of species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Fu, Z. Z. et al. Molecular data and ecological niche modeling reveal population dynamics of widespread shrub Forsythia suspensa (Oleaceae) in Chinaâs warm-temperate zone in response to climate change during the Pleistocene. BMC Evol. Biol.14, 114 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4090e611">25</a></sup>. We focused on the effects of gene flow, genetic drift, population demographic history, and geological history<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Hamrick, J. L. &amp; Godt, M. J. Plant Population Genetics, Breeding, and Genetic Resources (Sinauer, Sunderland, 1990)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4090e615">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Hewitt, G. M. Genetic consequences of climatic oscillations in the quaternary. Philos. Trans. R. Soc. Lond. B Biol. Sci.359, 183â195 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4090e618">27</a></sup>. However, natural selection generated along environmental gradients is also an important factor affecting the genetic structure. Increasing evidence of correlations between population genetic structure and environmental factors indicates a need to examine the role of natural selection in shaping the spatial structure of populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Manel, S. &amp; Holderegger, R. Ten years of landscape genetics. Trends Ecol. Evol.28, 614â621 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4090e622">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Balkenhol, N. et al. Current status, future opportunities, and remaining challenges in landscape genetics. In (eds Balkenhol, N. C., et al.). Landscape Genetics: Concepts, Methods, Applications (Wiley, Hoboken, 2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4090e625">29</a></sup>. In addition, natural selection caused by environmental factors will also lead to the differentiation of loci around these adaptive genes due to the hitchhiking effect, which will also influence the apparent relationships between environmental factors and genetic structure. However, we do not know whether the magnification of this effect will outweigh the effects of other neutral processes, such as gene flow and drift. When molecular markers are used for analyses at the genome level, the role of natural selection in determining population genetic structure will be more readily measurable. Overall, the use of population genomics is helpful for understanding the genetic mechanisms of local species adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Cushman, S. A. et al. Editorial: the least cost path from landscape genetics to landscape genomics: challenges and opportunities to explore NGS data in a spatially explicit context. Front. Genet.9, 215 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4090e629">2</a></sup>.</p><p>Weeping forsythia, <i>F. suspensa</i> (Thunb.) Vahl (Oleaceae) (2<i>n</i>â=â2<i>x</i>â=â28), is a dominant, deciduous shrub that is widely distributed in the warm temperate zone in China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Fu, Z. Z. et al. Molecular data and ecological niche modeling reveal population dynamics of widespread shrub Forsythia suspensa (Oleaceae) in Chinaâs warm-temperate zone in response to climate change during the Pleistocene. BMC Evol. Biol.14, 114 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4090e645">25</a></sup>. In previous phylogeographical studies of <i>F. suspensa</i>, it was inferred that this species tracked quaternary climatic changes by expanding to nearby low-elevation plains in glacial periods and retreating to mountaintops during interglacial warm periods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Fu, Z. Z. et al. Molecular data and ecological niche modeling reveal population dynamics of widespread shrub Forsythia suspensa (Oleaceae) in Chinaâs warm-temperate zone in response to climate change during the Pleistocene. BMC Evol. Biol.14, 114 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4090e653">25</a></sup>. This suggests that the species has responded to environmental fluctuation through a combination of limited local range shifts, local adaptation, and phenotypic plasticity. Thus, this species may be a good candidate for studies of local adaptation given the long period of environmental selection it has experienced within a relatively fixed range compared to other species that experienced more extensive range shifts following climate fluctuations. Thus, we believe <i>F. suspensa</i> is a good model for inferring local adaptation.</p><p>A recent study on <i>F. suspensa</i> indicated that the seasonal variations in precipitation, the extreme lowest temperature, and precipitation in the wettest month play important roles in the process of population adaptive differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yang, J. et al. Landscape population genomics of forsythia (Forsythia suspensa) reveal that ecological habitats determine the adaptive evolution of species. Front. Plant Sci.8, 481 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4090e666">30</a></sup>. However, whether these environmental gradients are key factors explaining genomic structure still needs to be verified. Furthermore, the identity of the genes associated with the observed adaptive differentiation is still not known because of the anonymous molecular markers used in previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yang, J. et al. Landscape population genomics of forsythia (Forsythia suspensa) reveal that ecological habitats determine the adaptive evolution of species. Front. Plant Sci.8, 481 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4090e670">30</a></sup>. In addition, Yang et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yang, J. et al. Landscape population genomics of forsythia (Forsythia suspensa) reveal that ecological habitats determine the adaptive evolution of species. Front. Plant Sci.8, 481 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4090e674">30</a></sup> found that most adaptive loci (22 out of 23) are simultaneously related to multiple environmental variables, indicating substantial pleiotropy. This may indicate that most of the adaptive differentiation occurs in the genes related to crosstalk between different environmental factors.</p><p>In this study, we explored the genetic mechanisms of the local adaptation of <i>F. suspensa</i> using population genomics data from specific-locus amplified fragment sequencing (SLAF)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Sun, X. et al. SLAF-seq: an efficient method of large-scale de novo SNP discovery and genotyping using high-throughput sequencing. PLoS ONE8, e58700 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d4090e684">31</a></sup>. To better understand this mechanism, we sequenced the weeping forsythia genome using Nanopore PromethION. To date, the genomes of three species in Oleaceae have been published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sollars, E. S. A. et al. Genome sequence and genetic diversity of European ash trees. Nature541, 212â216 (2017)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d4090e688">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Unver, T. et al. Genome of wild olive and the evolution of oil biosynthesis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA114, E9413âE9422 (2017)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4090e688_1">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Yang, X. et al. The chromosome-level quality genome provides insights into the evolution of the biosynthesis genes for aroma compounds of Osmanthus fragrans. Hortic. Res.5, 72 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4090e691">34</a></sup>. The genome sequencing of <i>F. suspensa</i> will be useful for understanding the structure of Oleaceae genomes in general and the evolution of the Oleaceae family. In this study, our main objective was to address the following questions: (a) what is the genomic basis underlying local adaption? (b) can more adaptive differentiation be seen in the genes related to signal crosstalk between different environmental modules? and (c) is natural selection resulting from these environmental variables is sufficient to explain the observed population genetic structure of this species?</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Assembly and annotation of the <i>F. suspensa</i> genome</h3><p>As <i>F. suspensa</i> is a nonmodel species without a reference genome, we employed both the Illumina and Nanopore platforms to perform de novo genome assembly. To estimate the genome size of <i>F. suspensa</i>, we performed preliminary sequencing using the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform to survey the genome. A total of 64.39âGb of data were generated on the HiSeq 2500 platform (Illumina, USA). The <i>K</i>-mer spectrum analysis suggested an estimated genome size of 701.40âMb. With this background, we assembled the reference genome of <i>F. suspensa</i> using 113Ã Nanopore ultralong reads. After correction using Illumina reads, we obtained an integrated assembly of 737.5âMb in size, which consists of a total of 1214 contigs, with a contig N50 of 7.3âMb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#Tab1">1</a>). Both the BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs; 91.9% of 1440 BUSCOs) and CEGMA (Core Eukaryotic Genes Mapping Approach; 97.2% of CEGs) assessments indicated high completeness of the assembled genome. We annotated 401.7âMb (54.5% of the total length) of repetitive sequences in the <i>F. suspensa</i> genome according to repeat prediction, with the <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> families representing the most abundant transposable elements in the <i>F. suspensa</i> genome (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S1</a>). Based on a combination of <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction, homologous identification and transcripts derived from various tissues, we annotated 33,062 protein-coding genes (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">2</a>: Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S1</a> and Supplementary File 1: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S2</a>). Approximately, 33.0â96.0% of the total annotated genes were found to be homologous to known proteins in the translated EMBL (TrEMBL) database, 96.2% in the nonredundant (NR) database, 54.7% in the eukaryotic orthologous groups (KOG) database, and 50.1% in the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) database, and homologs were also found in other databases (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S3</a>). In addition, we annotated the noncoding RNA genes in the assembly, among which 103 microRNAs, 219 ribosomal RNAs, and 681 transfer RNAs were predicted (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of the <i>Forsythia suspensa</i> genome assembly and annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">SNP identification and quality control</h3><p>To provide a genome-wide overview of the genomic dynamics underlying local adaptation, a total of 300 <i>F. suspensa</i> individuals were collected from 15 natural populations across its current distribution range in China (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#Fig1">1a</a>). Based on these population samples, our reduced-representation genome sequencing approach yielded 1,120,232 SLAF loci, with an average sequencing depth of 11.51Ã for each SLAF locus. Among these SLAF loci, 1,021,768 were polymorphic, among which 575,792 high-quality SNPs (allele frequencyâ&gt;â0.05 and integrityâ&gt;â0.8) were used for subsequent population genetic analyses.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Gene flow between groups and sampling localities of Forsythia suspensa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Gene flow between groups and sampling localities of <i>Forsythia suspensa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00352-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_352_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00352-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_352_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="278" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Sampling localities of 15 populations of <i>F. suspensa</i>, where colors distinguish groups; <b>b</b> maximum-likelihood tree and migration events among five groups of <i>F. suspensa</i>. The migration events are colored according to their weight</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Phylogenetic and population genomic analyses</h3><p>The population genetic structure of <i>F. suspensa</i> was inferred using ADMIXTURE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Alexander, D. H., Novembre, J. &amp; Lange, K. Fast model-based estimation of ancestry in unrelated individuals. Genome Res.19, 1655â1664 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1091">35</a></sup> based on all high-quality SNPs. Optimal ancestral clustering at <i>K</i>â=â5 was selected based on the cross-validation error rate (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">2</a>: Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S2</a>). The resulting ancestral inference of the populations was broadly consistent with the geographic origin of the 15 populations. Thus, we defined these natural populations as the following five groups: (1) northwest group (NWG): populations SXWL and SXHM; (2) southwest group (SWG), populations SXLJ, HBWD, HNLY, and HNLJ; (3) eastern group (EG), populations SDBD, SDTM, and SDMM; (4) northern group (NG), populations SXLK, HBWZ, HNJL, and HNSM; and (5) southern group (SG), populations HNTB and HNJG (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#Fig2">2a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Population genetic structure of Forsythia suspensa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Population genetic structure of <i>Forsythia suspensa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00352-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_352_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00352-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_352_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="355" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Population genetic structure based on all SNPs estimated by ADMIXTURE analysis with <i>K</i>â=â5. Each bar represents an individual, and the <i>x</i>-axis location specifies the sampling location. <b>b</b> The phylogenetic tree of all individuals of <i>F. suspensa</i> with <i>F. viridissima</i> and <i>J. nudiflorum</i> as outgroups was constructed using the neighbor-joining method in the program MEGA. <b>c</b> Each point represents an individual genotype, where colors distinguish groups. The amount of variation explained by PC1, PC2, and PC3 is 5.14%, 1.76% and 1.39%, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also reconstructed the phylogenetic relationships of the 15 populations based on the same dataset. Most results were consistent with the population structure detailed above; however, the SG group was further divided into two subgroups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#Fig2">2b</a>). Principal component analysis (PCA) also illustrated 5 distinct groups among the 15 natural populations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#Fig2">2c</a>). Notably, while all the above population genomic inferences suggested a clear spatial structure of <i>F. suspensa</i>, a relatively small proportion of the genetic variation (11.20%, <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>â=â0.112, <i>P</i>â&gt;â0.05; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#Tab2">2</a>) could be explained by intergroup divergence. Combining the three clustering methods, PCA and ADMIXTURE based on the Bayesian algorithm showed a much clearer population structure than the phylogenetic tree. To infer the environmental correlates with the genetic structure, we performed another ADMIXTURE analysis based on the neutral loci (i.e., excluding outlier loci). The optimal clustering results of the population changed to <i>K</i>â=â4 (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">2</a>: Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S3</a>). The results of Treemix analysis showed that extensive gene flow occurred between EG and other groups, whereas there was a lack of detectable gene flow between EG and SG (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#Fig1">1b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 AMOVAs for SNP variation surveyed in populations of <i>Forsythia suspensa</i> in China</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Characterization of candidate environment-associated loci (EAL)</h3><p>Based on the genome-wide scan for signatures of selection, 8629 unique SNPs that possessed a posterior probability over 0.76 and <i>q</i> value under 0.05 were defined as outlier loci (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S5</a>). To further examine whether these loci were associated with environmental variables, both the LFMM and SamÎ²ada approaches were employed. Before the association analysis, PCA was performed to eliminate collinearity among the 43 environmental variables. Three PCs explaining 94.6% of the total variation were retained for subsequent environmental association analyses. In PC1 (56.5% of variance), the temperature variables Bio1, Bio6, Bio9, and Bio11, the precipitation variables Bio12, Bio14, Bio17, and Bio19, and the water vapor pressure variables vapr1âvapr12 showed strong negative correlations with loadings under â0.8. In PC2 (30.1% of variance), the solar radiation variables Sr8âSr11 were strongly negatively correlated with loadings under â0.8 (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S6</a>). All the above environmental variables are shown in the Supplementary material (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S7</a>). As a result, a total of 722 and 6340 SNPs associated with the transformed environmental variables were identified by LFMM and SamÎ²ada, respectively (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">2</a>: Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S5</a>; Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S9</a>). To reduce the false discovery rate, only 524 mutual SNPs that were identified by both approaches were considered EAL (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S10</a>). Redundancy analysis (RDA) was then performed to examine the environmental variables related to the genetic variation of these 524 candidate loci. Axes 1 and 2 of the RDA explained 67.13% and 27.99% of the variance in the 524 candidate EAL, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#Fig4">4</a>). Our analyses also revealed that the PC1 and PC2 transformed environmental variables were strongly associated with RDA axes 2 and 1 (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S11</a>), indicating that the two RDA axes could explain the majority of the changes in the three transformed environmental variables. PC1 represented most of the variables associated with low temperature during the cold season, the annual mean temperature, precipitation during the dry season, annual precipitation, and annual air humidity. PC2 represented solar radiation from August to November (i.e., solar radiation during the period of fruit development and seed drying after ripening). These attributes indicated that PC1 and PC2 might play important roles in the adaptive genetic differentiation of <i>F. suspensa</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Manhattan plots showing signatures of selection in the BayeScan results."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Manhattan plots showing signatures of selection in the BayeScan results.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" with a cut-off value of a posterior probability above 0.76 and <i>q</i> value below 0.05</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button"
