contribution of each environmental variable shaping the spatial genetic structure.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00352-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_352_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00352-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_352_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="626" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The biplot depicts the eigenvalues and lengths of eigenvectors for the RDA</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To further understand the functions of these identified EALs, we annotated the 524 identified SNPs using our assembled genome. A total of 1932 candidate adaptive genes were annotated by referring to our assembled genome (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S12</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Oleaceae is a medium-sized family containing approximately 600 species from 25 genera<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Wallander, E. &amp; Albert, V. A. Phylogeny and classification of Oleaceae based on rps16 and trnL-F sequence data. Am. J. Bot.87, 1827â1841 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1514">36</a></sup>. Many of these genera are economically important, such as <i>Fraxinus</i>, <i>Forsythia</i>, <i>Jasminum</i>, <i>Ligustrum</i>, <i>Olea</i>, <i>Osmanthus</i>, and <i>Syringa</i>. Currently, genomes are available for three species of the family: <i>F. excelsior</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Sollars, E. S. A. et al. Genome sequence and genetic diversity of European ash trees. Nature541, 212â216 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1542">32</a></sup>, <i>O. europaea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Unver, T. et al. Genome of wild olive and the evolution of oil biosynthesis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA114, E9413âE9422 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1548">33</a></sup>, and <i>O. fragrans</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Yang, X. et al. The chromosome-level quality genome provides insights into the evolution of the biosynthesis genes for aroma compounds of Osmanthus fragrans. Hortic. Res.5, 72 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1555">34</a></sup>. Here, we present a high-quality reference genome sequence of the widespread species <i>F. suspensa</i>, an important medicinal and ornamental plant. Indicating its importance as a medicinal plant, most traditional Chinese medicines for influenza treatment contain an extract of <i>F. suspensa</i> fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wang, T. Q. et al. TCM treatment of anemopyretic cold rule analysis. J. Tianjin Univ. Tradit. Chin. Med.37, 113â117 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1565">37</a></sup>. It is also one of the main traditional Chinese medicines used in the recent treatment of COVID-19. This species has been widely cultivated in China in recent years. However, the varieties of this species under cultivation are very limited. The obtained genome information for <i>F. suspensa</i> will therefore be an essential resource for biological research and breeding. Here, we used genomic information in combination with a landscape genomics approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Cushman, S. A. et al. Editorial: the least cost path from landscape genetics to landscape genomics: challenges and opportunities to explore NGS data in a spatially explicit context. Front. Genet.9, 215 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1573">2</a></sup> to explore the genetic basis of the local adaptation of <i>F. suspensa</i>.</p><p>Naturally distributed species experience complex, heterogeneous environments, and different environmental variables exert differential selection pressure on the associated populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Li, J. X. et al. Adaptive genetic differentiation in Pterocarya stenoptera (Juglandaceae) driven by multiple environmental variables were revealed by landscape genomics. BMC Plant Biol.18, 306 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1583">18</a></sup>. When species experience selection pressures related to multiple environmental factors, the genomes of different populations will undergo complex adaptive differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Rellstab, C. et al. A practical guide to environmental association analysis in landscape genomics. Mol. Ecol.24, 4348â4370 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1587">4</a></sup>. Here, a total of 524 SNPs with signatures of natural selection along environmental gradients were identified by both LFMM and SamÎ²ada. To genomically identify these loci, we annotated them in our assembled genome. According to the standard of Fritsche et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Fritsche, L. G. et al. A large genome-wide association study of age-related macular degeneration highlights contributions of rare and common variants. Nat. Genet.48, 134â143 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1591">38</a></sup>, we searched the candidate adaptive genes in the Â±100âkb SNPs of EAL. Here, we annotated a total of 1932 candidate adaptive genes.</p><p>To test the hypothesis that more adaptive differentiation will occur in the genes related to signal crosstalk between different environmental modules as a result of local adaptation to heterogeneous environmental factors, we investigated a total of 640 genes annotated by using KEGG and found multiple examples that are likely related to local adaptation (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S13</a>). In our analysis, we used statistically independent composite environmental variables. PC1 represents the environmental variables related to temperature and water. We found that 286 out of 640 genes were considered candidate adaptive genes associated with PC1 based on the LFMM results. Among these genes, 87 genes were confirmed to be associated with environmental adaptation in other species and were involved in the activation or suppression of transcription, the synthesis of oxidoreductase, hormones and fatty acids, electron transport in the photosynthetic system, and other pathways.</p><p>Consistent with our hypothesis, most of these genes (56 out of 87 genes) were adaptively differentiated with respect to both temperature and water stress, suggesting that the crosstalk observed between the two modules might be a common phenomenon during the process of local adaptation. For example, APETALA2-like ethylene-responsive transcription factors (EVM0000677.1, EVM0023986.1, EVM0021041.1, and EVM0019895.1) were identified as being under divergent selection in PC1. This plant-specific transcription factor has been shown to play an important role in regulating plant growth and responses to cold, heat, salt, drought and flooding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Najafi, S., Sorkheh, K. &amp; Nasernakhaei, F. Characterization of the APETALA2/Ethylene-responsive factor (AP2/ERF) transcription factor family in sunflower. Sci. Rep.8, 11576 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1607">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Xie, Z. et al. AP2/ERF transcription factor regulatory networks in hormone and abiotic stress responses in Arabidopsis. Front. Plant Sci.10, 228 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1610">40</a></sup>. Peroxidase (EVM0032298.1), an enzyme that catalyzes the oxidation of a particular substrate by hydrogen peroxide, was differentially expressed under drought and low temperature stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Chakraborty, U. &amp; Pradhan, B. Drought stress-induced oxidative stress and antioxidative responses in four wheat (Triticum aestivum L.) varieties. Arch. Agron. Soil Sci.58, 617â630 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1614">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Noureddine, Y. Changes of peroxidase activities under cold stress in annuals populations of medicago. Mol. Plant Breed.6, 5 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1617">42</a></sup>. Gibberellin 3-beta-dioxygenase (EVM0027506.1), one of the key enzymes involved in gibberellin synthesis, showed significant differential expression under low temperature and drought conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Gong, L. et al. Transcriptome profiling of the potato (Solanum tuberosum L.) plant under drought stress and water-stimulus conditions. PLoS ONE10, e0128041 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1621">43</a></sup>, and its sequence differentiation might suggest that it differentially regulates plant growth through the gibberellin pathway to adapt to different environmental conditions. 3-Ketoacyl-CoA synthase (EVM0017367.1) participates in very-long-chain fatty acid synthesis and plays a role in wax biosynthesis. A previous study of <i>Brassica napus</i> suggested that the overexpression of the genes encoding 3-ketoacyl-CoA synthase promotes cuticular wax production and increases drought tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Wang, M. et al. Comparative transcriptome analysis to elucidate the enhanced thermotolerance of tea plants (Camellia sinensis) treated with exogenous calcium. Planta249, 775â786 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1628">44</a></sup>. Furthermore, Wang et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Wang, M. et al. Comparative transcriptome analysis to elucidate the enhanced thermotolerance of tea plants (Camellia sinensis) treated with exogenous calcium. Planta249, 775â786 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1633">44</a></sup> found that the expression of 3-ketoacyl-CoA synthase genes was significantly downregulated by cold stress, which also indicated that they might be associated with cold adaptation. Cytochrome b6âf complex subunit 4 (EVM0014903.1), a subunit of cytochrome b6-f, plays a role in photosynthesis by transferring electrons between photosystems II and I in plants. Previous results showed the repressed accumulation of the cytochrome b6âf complex under drought stress, which suggests that it is related to drought adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="SchÃ¶ttler, M. A. et al. Photosynthetic complex stoichiometry dynamics in higher plants: biogenesis, function, and turnover of ATP synthase and the cytochrome b6f complex. J. Exp. Bot.66, 2373â2400 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1637">45</a></sup>. As most of the identified divergent genes with indirect evidence in other species were simultaneously differentiated in relation to the temperature stress and water stress modules, we speculate that this might indicate that the adaptive differentiation of the genes related to crosstalk between two modules is more efficient than that of other genes. This might also be due to the climatic characteristics of this region. To verify this hypothesis, further functional verification of these divergent genes with unknown functions in temperature stress and water stress modules is still needed.</p><p>PC2 represents the solar radiation from August to November. We found that 403 out of 640 genes showed adaptive differentiation associated with PC2 based on the LFMM results. A total of 61 of these genes had been previously confirmed to be involved in the light module in other species (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S13</a>). Based on previous functional annotations, we found that these divergent genes in the light module were related to light stress, photosynthesis, photoprotection and photomorphogenesis. For example, homogentisate phytyltransferase (EVM0012785.1) is a key enzyme limiting tocopherol biosynthesis. High-light stress could result in a significant elevation of total tocopherol levels and might provide an additional line of defense against oxidative damage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Collakova, E. &amp; DellaPenna, D. The role of homogentisate phytyltransferase and other tocopherol pathway enzymes in the regulation of tocopherol synthesis during abiotic stress. Plant Physiol.133, 930â940 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1651">46</a></sup>. Manganese transport protein (EVM0021805.1) is a protein located at the membrane of cells that pumps out manganese. Manganese is an essential plant mineral nutrient and plays a key role in photosynthesis, and manganese deficiency could result in a decrease in chlorophyll content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Gavalas, N. A. &amp; Clark, H. E. On the role of manganese in photosynthesis: kinetics of photoinhibition in manganese-deficent and 3-(4-chlorophenyl)-1, 1-dimethylurea-inhibited Euglena gracilis. Plant Physiol.47, 139â143 (1971)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1655">47</a></sup>. Jasmonic acid-amino synthetase (EVM0011590.1) activates the conjugation between jasmonate and various amino acids, and overexpression of this enzyme causes a far-red specific hyperphotomorphogenic response<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Chen, C. Y. et al. Structural basis of jasmonate-amido synthetase FIN219 in complex with glutathione S-transferase FIP1 during the JA signal regulation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA114, E1815âE1824 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1659">48</a></sup>. The carotenoid cleavage dioxygenases (EVM0011590.1), a family of non-heme iron-containing dioxygenase enzymes, catalyze the oxidative cleavage of carotenoid substrates. Carotenoids participate in various biological processes in plants, such as photosynthesis, photomorphogenesis, photoprotection, and development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Nisar, N. et al. Carotenoid metabolism in plant. Mol. Plant8, 68â82 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1664">49</a></sup>.</p><p>Interestingly, we found 107 candidate adaptive genes that were related to both PC1 and PC2 based on the LFMM results. According to the results of previous studies, 24 out of these 107 genes were considered candidate adaptive genes that were simultaneously involved in the adaptation to light and temperature and/or water stress modules (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S13</a>). Of course, as this general inference is based on from indirect evidence from other species, we cannot completely exclude the possible existence of false-positive signals of natural selection caused by the hitchhiking effect. Thus, the candidate adaptive genes that we identified in this study require further functional verification. Importantly, however, our results strongly suggest that the adaptive differentiation of genes related to multiple metabolic pathways occurred in the genome of natural plant populations under multiple abiotic selection pressures. Our study gives us substantial insight into the genetic basis of the local adaptation of <i>F. suspensa</i>, which should form the foundation for more thorough confirmatory work in common garden experiments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cushman, S. A. Grand challenges in evolutionary and population genetics: the importance of integrating epigenetics, genomics, modeling, and experimentation. Front. Genet.5, 197 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1680">9</a></sup> and simulation modeling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Landguth, E. L. et al. Modeling multilocus selection in an individual-based, spatially-explicit landscape genetics framework. Mol. Ecol. Resour.20, 605â615 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1684">50</a></sup>.</p><p>In previous population genomics studies of <i>F. suspensa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yang, J. et al. Landscape population genomics of forsythia (Forsythia suspensa) reveal that ecological habitats determine the adaptive evolution of species. Front. Plant Sci.8, 481 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1693">30</a></sup>, it was not possible to identify the loci responsible for adaptive differentiation due to the limitations of molecular markers. However, the key environmental factors involved in the adaptive differentiation of <i>F. suspensa</i> have been identified. Waterlogging, cold, and drought are considered to be the most important environmental factors affecting the adaptive differentiation of <i>F. suspensa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yang, J. et al. Landscape population genomics of forsythia (Forsythia suspensa) reveal that ecological habitats determine the adaptive evolution of species. Front. Plant Sci.8, 481 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1702">30</a></sup>. In this study, RDA at the genome level confirmed that cold and drought significantly contributed to the adaptive differentiation of natural populations of <i>F. suspensa</i>, but waterlogging was ruled out as a significant contributor in this analysis, although we also found some adaptive genes associated with this phenomenon, such as malate dehydrogenase (EVM0007696.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Ram, S. Role of alcohol dehydrogenase, malate dehydrogenase and malic enzyme in flooding tolerance in Brachiaria Species. J. Plant Biochem. Biot.9, 45â47 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1710">51</a></sup> and gibberellin 3-beta-dioxygenase (EVM0027506.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Butsayawarapat, P. et al. Comparative transcriptome analysis of waterlogging-sensitive and tolerant zombi pea (Vigna vexillata) reveals energy conservation and root plasticity controlling waterlogging tolerance. Plants8, 264 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1714">52</a></sup>. In addition, we identified a new environmental factor that might play a key role in the adaptive differentiation of <i>F. suspensa</i>: solar radiation during the period of fruit development and seed drying after ripening, which has not been reported in previous studies.</p><p>Additional important questions are whether, when and how natural selection introduced by environmental variables will affect population genetic structure. The genetic structure of a species is affected by many factors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Ohsawa, T. &amp; Ide, Y. Global patterns of genetic variation in plant species along vertical and horizontal gradients on mountains. Glob. Ecol. Biogeogr.17, 152â163 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1724">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Yang, J. et al. Landscape genomics analysis of Achyranthes bidentata reveal adaptive genetic variations are driven by environmental variations relating to ecological habit. Popul. Ecol.59, 355â362 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1727">54</a></sup>. The identification of population genetic structure at the genome level can help provide a comprehensive understanding of the effects of these factors on population genetic structure. Recently, three studies have described the population genetic structure of <i>F. suspensa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Fu, Z. Z. et al. Molecular data and ecological niche modeling reveal population dynamics of widespread shrub Forsythia suspensa (Oleaceae) in Chinaâs warm-temperate zone in response to climate change during the Pleistocene. BMC Evol. Biol.14, 114 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1733">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yang, J. et al. Landscape population genomics of forsythia (Forsythia suspensa) reveal that ecological habitats determine the adaptive evolution of species. Front. Plant Sci.8, 481 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1736">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Fu, Z. Z. et al. Population genetics of the widespread shrub Forsythia suspensa (Oleaceae) in warm-temperate China using microsatellite loci: implication for conservation. Plant Syst. Evol.302, 1â9 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1739">55</a></sup>. The first study of the phylogeography of <i>F. suspensa</i> was based on chloroplast DNA (cpDNA) and nuclear ribosomal DNA (nrDNA). Importantly, cpDNA-based analyses divide the population of <i>F. suspensa</i> into seven groups, whereas nrDNA cannot separate these populations at all<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Fu, Z. Z. et al. Molecular data and ecological niche modeling reveal population dynamics of widespread shrub Forsythia suspensa (Oleaceae) in Chinaâs warm-temperate zone in response to climate change during the Pleistocene. BMC Evol. Biol.14, 114 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1749">25</a></sup>. The second study of population genetics in <i>F. suspensa</i> used microsatellite markers, in which the populations of <i>F. suspensa</i> that were not separated using nrDNA were divided into two groups, the east and west groups<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Jain, M. et al. Nanopore sequencing and assembly of a human genome with ultra-long reads. Nat. Biotechnol.36, 338â345 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1760">57</a></sup>. The third study of landscape genomics in <i>F. suspensa</i> used start codon-targeted (SCoT) polymorphism markers and further subdivided the western group identified by microsatellite markers into three groups<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yang, J. et al. Landscape population genomics of forsythia (Forsythia suspensa) reveal that ecological habitats determine the adaptive evolution of species. Front. Plant Sci.8, 481 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1767">30</a></sup>. The first two studies using nuclear molecular markers suggested that gene flow plays an important role in the population genetic structure of <i>F. suspensa</i>, while the third study using SCoT makers, a kind of nuclear gene-targeted marker, suggested that natural selection also plays an important role in shaping the population genetic structure of <i>F. suspensa</i>.</p><p>Here, our SLAF sequencing results provided 0.58 million high-quality SNPs across the genome, which enabled us to identify finer-scale population structures than in previous studies. Using these SNPs, regardless of their neutrality, our analysis provides a more comprehensive understanding of population genetic structure. Compared with phylogenetic tree methods, ADMIXTURE and PCA revealed a more refined population genetic structure of <i>F. suspensa</i>. As indicated through population genomic inferences, the 15 natural populations of <i>F. suspensa</i> could be broadly divided into 5 groups corresponding to their geographic locations. Notably, while both the PCA and ADMIXTURE inferences supported the genetic divergence of the five groups, polyphyletic relationships were observed in the NJ tree. There are three possible explanations for this phenomenon: (1) as indicated in the AMOVA, only a small proportion (11.2%) of the identified genetic variants contributed to the observed interpopulation genetic differentiation; (2) the Bayesian-based inference suggested that the <i>F. suspensa</i> populations within the same geographic group differed in their genetic constitution; and (3) historical gene flow occurred frequently among the five geographic groups. Consistent with the results of previous studies, we found that EG was separated from the other populations, and previous studies confirmed that this separation was caused by ecological isolation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Fu, Z. Z. et al. Molecular data and ecological niche modeling reveal population dynamics of widespread shrub Forsythia suspensa (Oleaceae) in Chinaâs warm-temperate zone in response to climate change during the Pleistocene. BMC Evol. Biol.14, 114 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1790">25</a></sup>. However, the identification of other subgroups in this study differed from the results of previous studies.</p><p>To understand the contribution of natural selection imposed by environmental variables to the population genetic structure of <i>F. suspensa</i>, we conducted a comparison between the clustering results of all SNP loci and the results excluding outlier loci. After excluding outlier loci, the east group was still separated from the other groups, whereas the clustering results of the other groups changed significantly. This further confirmed that the separation of the EG from the other populations is not caused by natural selection, but natural selection plays an important role in shaping the genetic structure of other populations of <i>F. suspensa</i>. These results are consistent with previous landscape genomics studies on <i>F. suspensa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yang, J. et al. Landscape population genomics of forsythia (Forsythia suspensa) reveal that ecological habitats determine the adaptive evolution of species. Front. Plant Sci.8, 481 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1805">30</a></sup>. Although the TREEMIX results showed gene flow between the EG and NWG, NG, and SG groups, the current amount of gene flow is not sufficient to eliminate the genetic differentiation caused by ecological isolation. In previous population genetics studies, we have paid more attention to the effects of genetic drift and gene flow on population genetic structure due to the use of neutral markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Ohsawa, T. &amp; Ide, Y. Global patterns of genetic variation in plant species along vertical and horizontal gradients on mountains. Glob. Ecol. Biogeogr.17, 152â163 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1809">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Yang, J. et al. Landscape genomics analysis of Achyranthes bidentata reveal adaptive genetic variations are driven by environmental variations relating to ecological habit. Popul. Ecol.59, 355â362 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1812">54</a></sup>. However, natural selection is also an important force shaping the population genetic structure of species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Miao, C. Y. et al. Landscape genomics reveal that ecological character determines adaptation: a case study in smoke tree (Cotinus coggygria Scop.). BMC Evol. Biol.17, 202 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4090e1816">17</a></sup>. It promotes the differentiation of adaptive genes in the genome of species among natural populations, and this genetic differentiation is further amplified due to the hitchhiking effect. This also amplifies the ability of natural selection to shape population genetic structure. Our study provides an empirical example of a speciesâ genetic structure being significantly influenced by natural selection imposed by multiple environmental factors.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>In the present study, we provide the first genome of <i>F. suspensa</i>, an important medicinal and ornamental plant. The genome information of <i>F. suspensa</i> is an essential resource for biological research and breeding in this species. We used this genome information in combination with a landscape genomics approach to explore the genetic basis of the local adaptation of this species. Our results showed that the genes of <i>F. suspensa</i> in the light, temperature and water modules have undergone adaptive differentiation under the effects of heterogeneous environmental gradients. We also found extensive signal crosstalk involving these adaptive genes between different modules. Among these environmental factors, solar radiation during the period of fruit development and seed drying after ripening, cold, and drought were found to significantly contribute to the adaptive differentiation of <i>F. suspensa</i>. Natural selection driven by multiple environmental factors has significantly contributed to the population genetic structure of <i>F. suspensa</i>. Overall, our study provides insights into the genetic mechanisms of local adaptation in this species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Sampling</h3><p>The specimen used for de novo assembly was collected from the Zhengzhou Botanical Garden (voucher no. 2018LIFS021). The 300 <i>F. suspensa</i> samples were collected from 15 geographically isolated locations (voucher No. 2018LIFS001â2018LIFS015) covering its current distribution range in China (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#Fig1">1a</a>; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#Tab3">3</a>). In addition, two samples of <i>Forsythia</i><i>viridissima</i> and <i>Jasminum nudiflorum</i> were collected from Henan Agricultural University. The population samples used for SLAF sequencing were placed in silica gel at room temperature after collection. All samples included in this study were used according to Chinese regulations. All voucher specimens were identified by Dr. Yong Li.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Details of the population locations of <i>Forsythia suspensa</i> sampled in China</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Genome sequencing and assembly, gene and repeat annotation</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from fresh young leaves by using plant DNA extraction kits (Tiangen, Beijing, China). DNA was quantified using a NanoDrop One microvolume UVâvis spectrophotometer (Thermo Fisher, Wilmington, USA). The genome size of <i>F. suspensa</i> was estimated based on the Illumina short insert library (350âbp) using Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Marcais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics27, 764â770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2349">56</a></sup>. The library for Nanopore PromethION sequencing was prepared according the protocol of Jain et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Jain, M. et al. Nanopore sequencing and assembly of a human genome with ultra-long reads. Nat. Biotechnol.36, 338â345 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2353">57</a></sup>. The concentration of the DNA library was assessed with a Qubit fluorometer (Thermo Fisher). After the construction of the library, the sample was added into the flow cell, and the flow cell was transferred to a Nanopore PromethION sequencer at Biomarker Technologies (Beijing, China) for real-time single-molecule sequencing to obtain original sequencing data. The read quality values of the original data sequenced on the Nanopore sequencing platform were preliminarily filtered to remove reads with low quality and a length of less than 2âkb. Reads after preliminary filtration were corrected and assembled using the program Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive Îº-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res.27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2357">58</a></sup>. Then, WTDBG2 and SMARTdenovo were used to assemble the error-corrected data from Canu. Quickmerge<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Chakraborty, M. et al. Contiguous and accurate de novo assembly of metazoan genomes with modest long read coverage. Nucleic Acids Res.44, e147 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2361">59</a></sup> was used to integrate the assembly results yielded by WTDBG2 and SMARTdenovo, and the integration results were corrected with Racon software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Vaser, R. et al. Fast and accurate de novo genome assembly from long uncorrected reads. Genome Res.27, 737â746 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2366">60</a></sup>. To improve the accuracy of the genome sequences of <i>F. suspensa</i>, we used the Illumina data to further calibrate the sequencing data of Nanopore PromethION. After correction with Pilon software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2373">61</a></sup>, the final version of the genome of <i>F. suspensa</i> was obtained. The genome completeness was assessed using a terrestrial plant dataset from BUSCO version 2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="SimÃ£o, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics31, 3210 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2380">62</a></sup> and a eukaryote dataset from CEGMA version 2.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2384">63</a></sup>. The protocol for gene and repeat annotation is provided in Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">2</a>: Protocol S1.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Genotyping by SLAF sequencing</h3><p>The genomic DNA of the 300 <i>F. suspensa</i> and four outgroup samples was extracted from the silica gel-dried leaf material using a Plant DNA Extraction Kit (Tiangen, Beijing, China). DNA was quantified with Nanodrop One UVâvis spectrophotometers (Thermo Fisher Scientific, Waltham, MA, USA). In this experiment, an improved SLAF sequencing strategy was used to construct the sequencing library. Using our own assembled genome as a reference for electron digestion prediction, two enzymes (HaeIII-Hpy166II, New England Biolabs, Ipswich, MA, USA) were selected for the digestion of genomic DNA. The SLAF-seq library was constructed using a customized version of Zhang et al.âs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Zhang, J. et al. High-density genetic map construction and identification of a locus controlling weeping trait in an ornamental woody plant (Prunus mume Sieb. et Zucc). DNA Res.22, 1â9 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2403">64</a></sup> protocol. Libraries were sequenced using 125-bp paired end reads on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform (Illumina, Inc., San Diego, CA, USA) at Biomarker Technologies (Beijing, China). The low-quality reads (quality scoreâ&lt;â30e) were removed, and the rest of the reads were sorted for each sample according to duplex barcode sequences. Clean reads from the same sample were mapped onto the genome sequence of <i>F. suspensa</i> using BWA software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2410">65</a></sup>. Sequences mapping to the same position with over 95% identity were considered to represent a single SLAF locus. Using GATK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res.20, 1297â1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2414">66</a></sup> and SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2419">67</a></sup> to develop SNP markers, the intersection of the SNPs obtained via the two methods was considered to represent the final reliable dataset. After filtering out the allele tags of each SLAF locus with a minor allele frequency under 5% and integrity under 80%, the remaining high-quality SNPs were screened for subsequent population genetic analyses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Population genetic analysis</h3><p>Population genomic analyses of the collected samples were performed based on PCA, admixture analysis, and phylogenetic inferences. PCA was performed by using EIGENSOFT version 6.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Price, A. L. et al. Principal components analysis corrects for stratification in genome-wide association studies. Nat. Genet.38, 904â909 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2431">68</a></sup>. To investigate the maximum likelihood of the ancestry of each individual, population structure was inferred by using ADMIXTURE version 1.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Yang, X. et al. The chromosome-level quality genome provides insights into the evolution of the biosynthesis genes for aroma compounds of Osmanthus fragrans. Hortic. Res.5, 72 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2435">34</a></sup> with a range of 1â10 coancestry clusters. The optimal <i>K</i> value was determined according to the valley value of the error rate. The genetic relationships of all samples of <i>F. suspensa</i> and the four outgroup samples were constructed using the neighbor-joining method in the program MEGA version 10.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Kumar, S. et al. MEGA X: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis across computing platforms. Mol. Biol. Evol.35, 1547â1549 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2445">69</a></sup>. The running parameters were set as follows: Kimura 2-parameter model with 1000 bootstrap replicates. According to the grouping information of ADMIXTURE, Arlequin version 3.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Excoffier, L. &amp; Lischer, H. E. L. Arlequin suite ver 3.5: a new series of programs to perform population genetics analyses under Linux and Windows. Mol. Ecol. Resour.10, 564â567 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2450">70</a></sup> was used to calculate the variation between individuals within and among all groups. Treemix version 1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Pickrell, J. K. &amp; Pritchard, J. K. Inference of population splits and mixtures from genome-wide allele frequency data. PLoS Genet.8, e1002967 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2454">71</a></sup> was used to infer population isolation and mixing patterns, and the direction and degree of gene flow were estimated based on the frequency variation of SNPs between different groups.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Environmental association analysis and functional annotation</h3><p>To identify the candidate environmental association loci (EAL), we identified outlier loci that were assumed under natural selection and detected them using BayeScan version 2.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Foll, M. &amp; Gaggiotti, O. E. A genome scan method to identify selected loci appropriate for both dominant and codominant markers: a Bayesian perspective. Genetics180, 977â993 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2467">72</a></sup>, with the following parameters: sample size of 5000, thinning interval of 10, 20 pilot runs with a 5000 run length, 50,000 burn-in iterations, and 10 prior odds. The cut-off thresholds were a posterior probability over 0.76 and a <i>q</i> value lower than 0.05, which are considered to provide substantial evidence of selection. Then, a total of 43 environmental variables (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S14</a>) recorded from 1970 to 2000 at a 2.5 arcmin resolution were used in this study, including 11 temperature variables, 8 precipitation variables, 12 solar radiation variables, and 12 water vapor pressure variables. All environmental variables were downloaded from Worldclim (<a href="http://www.diva-gis.org/climate">http://www.diva-gis.org/climate</a>) and extracted using DIVA-GIS 7.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Hijmans, R. J. et al. Computer tools for spatial analysis of plant genetic resources data: 1. DIVA-GIS. Plant Genet. Resour. Newsl.127, 15â19 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2491">73</a></sup>. To eliminate the collinearity of environmental variables, PCA was performed using the FactoMineR package in R. The original environmental data were standardized with the SCALE function prior to PCA. The first method was a latent factor mixed model (LFMM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Frichot, E. et al. Testing for associations between loci and environmental gradients using latent factor mixed models. Mol. Biol. Evol.30, 1687â1699 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2495">74</a></sup>, which was run with the R script of LFMM version 1.3. The running parameters were 100,000 sweeps and 50,000 burn-in sweeps. The number of latent factors was the optimal <i>K</i> value from the results of Admixture based on the neutral loci. The loci with |<i>z</i>| values over 4 and <i>P</i> values lower than 0.001 were identified as EAL. The second method was a logistic regression model run in SamÎ²ada version 0.8<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Joost, S. et al. A spatial analysis method (SAM) to detect candidate loci for selection: towards a landscape genomics approach to adaptation. Mol. Ecol.16, 3955â3969 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2509">75</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Stucki, S. et al. High performance computation of landscape genomic models including local indicators of spatial association. Mol. Ecol. Resour.17, 1072â1089 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2512">76</a></sup>. The cut-off value was set as a <i>P</i> value of the <i>G</i> score lower than 0.01 after Bonferroni correction. To infer the influence of these transformed environmental variables on adaptive genetic differentiation, RDA was performed using the vegan package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Oksanen, J. et al. Vegan: Community Ecology Package. R. Package Version 2.4-5 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2522">77</a></sup> in R. Here, the SNPs of EAL were used as response variables (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S10</a>), and the transformed environmental variables (the PC axes) (Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#MOESM1">S15</a>) were used as explanatory variables. For functional annotation, the candidate SNPs of EAL were annotated with our assembled genome of <i>F. suspensa</i> using the program BLAST X<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol.215, 403â410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2542">78</a></sup> with an <i>E</i> valueââ¤â10<sup>â5</sup>. To avoid missing candidate genes due to hitchhiking effects, the size of the sliding window for gene annotation was set as Â±100âkb for SNPs of EAL based on the standard of Fritsche et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Fritsche, L. G. et al. A large genome-wide association study of age-related macular degeneration highlights contributions of rare and common variants. Nat. Genet.48, 134â143 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00352-7#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d4090e2552">38</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Sequence data are archived at the National Center for Biotechnology Information (Information of SLAF sequencing: accession Nos. SRR10064062âSRR10064365, BioProject PRJNA563244; genome sequence information: accession No. WIPI00000000, BioProject PRJNA562106).</p>
